**Notes**

1.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_ref-259 "Jump up")** A situation is listed here if the Prosecutor of the Court has opened an investigation.
2.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_ref-260 "Jump up")** Obviously, only persons who are publicly indicted are listed. The Court can issue an indictment under seal.
3.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_ref-261 "Jump up")** If not otherwise noted, the indicted is wanted by warrant of arrest.
4.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_ref-262 "Jump up")** If there was a warrant of arrest, the dates of transfer to the International Criminal Court (_in italics_) and of the initial appearance are given. In case of a summons to appear, only the date of the initial appearance is given.
5.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_ref-Article-61-8-RS_271-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_ref-Article-61-8-RS_271-1) [<sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_ref-Article-61-8-RS_271-2) [<sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_ref-Article-61-8-RS_271-3) According to Article 61 (8) of the [Rome Statute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rome_Statute "Rome Statute"), "where the Pre-Trial Chamber declines to confirm a charge, the Prosecutor shall not be precluded from subsequently requesting its confirmation if the request is supported by additional evidence."

## Relationships

### United Nations

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Darfur_report_-_Page_2_Image_1.jpg/220px-Darfur_report_-_Page_2_Image_1.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Darfur_report_-_Page_2_Image_1.jpg)

The [UN Security Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council "United Nations Security Council") referred the [situation in Darfur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_in_Darfur "War in Darfur") to the ICC in 2005.

Unlike the [International Court of Justice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice "International Court of Justice"), the ICC is legally independent from the United Nations. The Rome Statute grants certain powers to the [United Nations Security Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council "United Nations Security Council"), which limit its functional independence. Article 13 allows the Security Council to refer to the Court situations that would not otherwise fall under the Court's jurisdiction (as it did in relation to the situations in Darfur and Libya, which the Court could not otherwise have prosecuted as neither Sudan nor Libya are state parties). Article 16 allows the Security Council to require the Court to defer from investigating a case for a period of twelve months.<sup id="cite_ref-RomeStatute_72-58"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-RomeStatute-72">[72]</a></sup><sup><span title="Page / location: article 16">: article 16 </span></sup>  Such a deferral may be renewed indefinitely by the Security Council. This sort of an arrangement gives the ICC some of the advantages inhering in the organs of the United Nations such as using the enforcement powers of the Security Council, but it also creates a risk of being tainted with the political controversies of the Security Council.<sup id="cite_ref-317"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-317">[294]</a></sup>

The Court cooperates with the UN in many different areas, including the exchange of information and logistical support.<sup id="cite_ref-un&amp;icc_318-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-un&amp;icc-318">[295]</a></sup> The Court reports to the UN each year on its activities,<sup id="cite_ref-un&amp;icc_318-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-un&amp;icc-318">[295]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-319"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-319">[296]</a></sup> and some meetings of the Assembly of States Parties are held at UN facilities. The relationship between the Court and the UN is governed by a "Relationship Agreement between the International Criminal Court and the United Nations".<sup id="cite_ref-320"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-320">[297]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-321"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-321">[298]</a></sup>

### Nongovernmental organizations

During the 1970s and 1980s, international human rights and humanitarian Nongovernmental Organizations (or NGOs) began to proliferate at exponential rates. Concurrently, the quest to find a way to punish international crimes shifted from being the exclusive responsibility of legal experts to being shared with [international human rights](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_human_rights_law "International human rights law") activism.

NGOs helped birth the ICC through advocacy and championing for the prosecution of perpetrators of crimes against humanity. NGOs closely monitor the organization's declarations and actions, ensuring that the work that is being executed on behalf of the ICC is fulfilling its objectives and responsibilities to civil society.<sup id="cite_ref-Schiff_322-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-Schiff-322">[299]</a></sup> According to Benjamin Schiff, "From the Statute Conference onward, the relationship between the ICC and the NGOs has probably been closer, more consistent, and more vital to the Court than have analogous relations between NGOs and any other international organization."

There are a number of NGOs working on a variety of issues related to the ICC. The NGO [Coalition for the International Criminal Court](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coalition_for_the_International_Criminal_Court "Coalition for the International Criminal Court") has served as a sort of umbrella for NGOs to coordinate with each other on similar objectives related to the ICC. The CICC has 2,500 member organizations in 150 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-CICC_323-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-CICC-323">[300]</a></sup> The original steering committee included representatives from the [World Federalist Movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Federalist_Movement/Institute_for_Global_Policy "World Federalist Movement/Institute for Global Policy"), the [International Commission of Jurists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Commission_of_Jurists "International Commission of Jurists"), [Amnesty International](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amnesty_International "Amnesty International"), the [Lawyers Committee for Human Rights](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lawyers_Committee_for_Human_Rights "Lawyers Committee for Human Rights"), [Human Rights Watch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch "Human Rights Watch"), [Parliamentarians for Global Action](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parliamentarians_for_Global_Action "Parliamentarians for Global Action"), and [No Peace Without Justice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/No_Peace_Without_Justice "No Peace Without Justice").<sup id="cite_ref-Schiff_322-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-Schiff-322">[299]</a></sup> Today, many of the NGOs with which the ICC cooperates are members of the CICC. These organizations come from a range of backgrounds, spanning from major international NGOs such as [Human Rights Watch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch "Human Rights Watch") and [Amnesty International](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amnesty_International "Amnesty International"), to smaller, more local organizations focused on peace and justice missions.<sup id="cite_ref-Schiff_322-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-Schiff-322">[299]</a></sup> Many work closely with states, such as the International Criminal Law Network, founded and predominantly funded by [the Hague](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Hague "The Hague") municipality and the Dutch Ministries of Defense and Foreign Affairs. The CICC also claims organizations that are themselves federations, such as the [International Federation of Human Rights Leagues](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Federation_of_Human_Rights_Leagues "International Federation of Human Rights Leagues") (FIDH).

CICC members subscribe to three principles that permit them to work under the umbrella of the CICC, so long as their objectives match them:

-   Promoting worldwide ratification and implementation of the Rome Statute of the ICC
-   Maintaining the integrity of the Rome Statute of the ICC, and
-   Ensuring the ICC will be as fair, effective and independent as possible<sup id="cite_ref-CICC_323-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-CICC-323">[300]</a></sup>

The NGOs that work under the CICC do not normally pursue agendas exclusive to the work of the Court, rather they may work for broader causes, such as general human rights issues, victims' rights, gender rights, rule of law, conflict mediation, and peace.<sup id="cite_ref-Schiff_322-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-Schiff-322">[299]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-324"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-324">[301]</a></sup> The CICC coordinates their efforts to improve the efficiency of NGOs' contributions to the Court and to pool their influence on major common issues. From the ICC side, it has been useful to have the CICC channel NGO contacts with the Court so that its officials do not have to interact individually with thousands of separate organizations.

NGOs have been crucial to the evolution of the ICC, as they assisted in the creation of the normative climate that urged states to seriously consider the Court's formation. Their legal experts helped shape the Statute, while their lobbying efforts built support for it. They advocate Statute ratification globally and work at expert and political levels within member states for passage of necessary domestic legislation. NGOs are greatly represented at meetings for the [Assembly of States Parties](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#Assembly), and they use the ASP meetings to press for decisions promoting their priorities.<sup id="cite_ref-Schiff_322-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-Schiff-322">[299]</a></sup> Many of these NGOs have reasonable access to important officials at the ICC because of their involvement during the Statute process. They are engaged in monitoring, commenting upon, and assisting in the ICC's activities.

The ICC often depends on NGOs to interact with local populations. The Registry Public Information Office personnel and Victims Participation and Reparations Section officials hold seminars for local leaders, professionals and the media to spread the word about the Court.<sup id="cite_ref-Schiff_322-5"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-Schiff-322">[299]</a></sup> These are the kinds of events that are often hosted or organized by local NGOs. Because there can be challenges with determining which of these NGOs are legitimate, CICC regional representatives often have the ability to help screen and identify trustworthy organizations.

NGOs are also "sources of criticism, exhortation and pressure upon" the ICC.<sup id="cite_ref-Schiff_322-6"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-Schiff-322">[299]</a></sup> The ICC heavily depends on NGOs for its operations. Although NGOs and states cannot directly impact the judicial nucleus of the organization, they can impart information on crimes, can help locate victims and witnesses, and can promote and organize victim participation. NGOs outwardly comment on the Court's operations, "push for expansion of its activities especially in the new justice areas of outreach in conflict areas, in victims' participation and reparations, and in upholding due-process standards and defense 'equality of arms' and so implicitly set an agenda for the future evolution of the ICC."<sup id="cite_ref-Schiff_322-7"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-Schiff-322">[299]</a></sup> The relatively uninterrupted progression of NGO involvement with the ICC may mean that NGOs have become repositories of more institutional historical knowledge about the ICC than its national representatives, and have greater expertise than some of the organization's employees themselves. While NGOs look to mold the ICC to satisfy the interests and priorities that they have worked for since the early 1990s, they unavoidably press against the limits imposed upon the ICC by the states that are members of the organization. NGOs can pursue their own mandates, irrespective of whether they are compatible with those of other NGOs, while the ICC must respond to the complexities of its own mandate as well as those of the states and NGOs.

Another issue has been that NGOs possess "exaggerated senses of their ownership over the organization and, having been vital to and successful in promoting the Court, were not managing to redefine their roles to permit the Court its necessary independence."<sup id="cite_ref-Schiff_322-8"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-Schiff-322">[299]</a></sup> Additionally, because there does exist such a gap between the large human rights organizations and the smaller peace-oriented organizations, it is difficult for ICC officials to manage and gratify all of their NGOs. "ICC officials recognize that the NGOs pursue their own agendas, and that they will seek to pressure the ICC in the direction of their own priorities rather than necessarily understanding or being fully sympathetic to the myriad constraints and pressures under which the Court operates."<sup id="cite_ref-Schiff_322-9"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-Schiff-322">[299]</a></sup> Both the ICC and the NGO community avoid criticizing each other publicly or vehemently, although NGOs have released advisory and cautionary messages regarding the ICC. They avoid taking stances that could potentially give the Court's adversaries, particularly the U.S., more motive to berate the organization.

## Criticism and opposition

### African states

In October 2016, after repeated claims that the court was biased against African states, [Burundi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Burundi "Burundi"), [South Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Africa "South Africa") and the [Gambia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gambia "Gambia") announced their withdrawals from the Rome Statute.<sup id="cite_ref-WPGambiaWD_325-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-WPGambiaWD-325">[302]</a></sup> Following [Gambia's presidential election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2016_Gambian_presidential_election "2016 Gambian presidential election") later that year, which ended the long rule of [Yahya Jammeh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yahya_Jammeh "Yahya Jammeh"), Gambia rescinded its withdrawal notification.<sup id="cite_ref-326"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-326">[303]</a></sup> A decision by the [High Court of South Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High_Court_of_South_Africa "High Court of South Africa") in early 2017 ruled that the attempted withdrawal was unconstitutional, as it had not been agreed by Parliament, prompting the South African government to inform the UN that it was revoking its decision to withdraw.<sup id="cite_ref-327"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-327">[304]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-328"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-328">[305]</a></sup>

### African accusations of Western imperialism

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/William_Samoei_Ruto.jpg/170px-William_Samoei_Ruto.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:William_Samoei_Ruto.jpg)

Kenyan politician [William Ruto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Ruto "William Ruto") in February 2013

The ICC has been accused of bias and as being a tool of Western [imperialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imperialism "Imperialism"), only punishing leaders from small, weak states while ignoring crimes committed by richer and more powerful states.<sup id="cite_ref-329"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-329">[306]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-330"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-330">[307]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-331"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-331">[308]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-332"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-332">[309]</a></sup> This sentiment has been expressed particularly by African leaders due to an alleged disproportionate focus of the Court on Africa, while it claims to have a global mandate. Until January 2016, all nine situations which the ICC had been investigating were in African countries.<sup id="cite_ref-333"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-333">[310]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-france24.com_334-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-france24.com-334">[311]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-335"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-335">[312]</a></sup>

African critics have suggested the ICC is acting as a neo-colonial force seeking to further empower Western political and extractive interests in Africa.".<sup id="cite_ref-mcdonald2019_336-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-mcdonald2019-336">[313]</a></sup> Scholar Awol Allo has described the court's underlying problem that has led to these challenges with Africa as not overt [racism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Racism "Racism"), but [Eurocentrism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurocentrism "Eurocentrism").<sup id="cite_ref-auto_5-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-auto-5">[5]</a></sup> Another analysis suggests that African states are motivated by concerns over Africa's place in world order: the problem is the sovereign inequality displayed by the ICC prosecutor's focus.<sup id="cite_ref-337"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-337">[314]</a></sup>

The prosecution of Kenyan Deputy President [William Ruto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Ruto "William Ruto") and President [Uhuru Kenyatta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uhuru_Kenyatta "Uhuru Kenyatta") (both charged before coming into office) led to the Kenyan parliament passing a motion calling for Kenya's withdrawal from the ICC, and the country called on the other 33 African states party to the ICC to withdraw their support, an issue which was discussed at a special [African Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_Union "African Union") (AU) summit in October 2013.

Though the ICC has denied the charge of disproportionately targeting African leaders, and claims to stand up for victims wherever they may be, Kenya was not alone in criticising the ICC. Sudanese President [Omar al-Bashir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Omar_al-Bashir "Omar al-Bashir") visited [Kenya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kenya "Kenya"), [South Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Africa "South Africa"), [China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China "China"), [Nigeria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nigeria "Nigeria"), [Saudi Arabia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saudi_Arabia "Saudi Arabia"), [United Arab Emirates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates "United Arab Emirates"), [Egypt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egypt "Egypt"), [Ethiopia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethiopia "Ethiopia"), [Qatar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qatar "Qatar") and several other countries despite an outstanding ICC warrant for his arrest but was not arrested; he said that the charges against him are "exaggerated" and that the ICC was a part of a "[Western](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_world "Western world") plot" against him. Ivory Coast's government opted not to transfer former first lady [Simone Gbagbo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simone_Gbagbo "Simone Gbagbo") to the court but to instead try her at home. Rwanda's ambassador to the African Union, Joseph Nsengimana, argued that "It is not only the case of Kenya. We have seen international justice become more and more a political matter." Ugandan President [Yoweri Museveni](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yoweri_Museveni "Yoweri Museveni") accused the ICC of "mishandling complex African issues". Ethiopian Prime Minister [Hailemariam Desalegn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hailemariam_Desalegn "Hailemariam Desalegn"), at the time AU chairman, told the UN General Assembly at the [General debate of the sixty-eighth session of the United Nations General Assembly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_debate_of_the_sixty-eighth_session_of_the_United_Nations_General_Assembly "General debate of the sixty-eighth session of the United Nations General Assembly"): "The manner in which the ICC has been operating has left a very bad impression in Africa. It is totally unacceptable."<sup id="cite_ref-338"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-338">[315]</a></sup>

### African Union (AU) withdrawal proposal

South African President [Jacob Zuma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacob_Zuma "Jacob Zuma") said the perceptions of the ICC as "unreasonable" led to the calling of the special AU summit on 13 October 2015. Botswana is a notable supporter of the ICC in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-339"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-339">[316]</a></sup> At the summit, the AU did not endorse the proposal for a collective withdrawal from the ICC due to lack of support for the idea.<sup id="cite_ref-340"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-340">[317]</a></sup> The summit concluded that serving heads of state should not be put on trial and that the Kenyan cases should be deferred. Ethiopian formerly Foreign Minister [Tedros Adhanom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tedros_Adhanom "Tedros Adhanom") said: "We have rejected the double standard that the ICC is applying in dispensing international justice."<sup id="cite_ref-341"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-341">[318]</a></sup> Despite these calls, the ICC went ahead with requiring William Ruto to attend his trial.<sup id="cite_ref-342"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-342">[319]</a></sup> The UNSC was then asked to consider deferring the trials of Kenyatta and Ruto for a year,<sup id="cite_ref-343"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-343">[320]</a></sup> but this was rejected.<sup id="cite_ref-344"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-344">[321]</a></sup> In November, the ICC's Assembly of State Parties responded to Kenya's calls for an exemption for sitting heads of state<sup id="cite_ref-345"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-345">[322]</a></sup> by agreeing to consider [amendments to the Rome Statute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amendments_to_the_Rome_Statute_of_the_International_Criminal_Court "Amendments to the Rome Statute of the International Criminal Court") to address the concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-346"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-346">[323]</a></sup>

On 7 October 2016, [Burundi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Burundi "Burundi") announced that it would leave the ICC, after the court began investigating political violence in that nation. In the two weeks that followed, South Africa and The Gambia also announced their intention to leave the court, with Kenya and Namibia reportedly also considering departure. All three nations cited the fact that all 39 people indicted by the court over its history by that date had been African and that the court has made no effort to investigate war crimes tied to the [2003 invasion of Iraq](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq "2003 invasion of Iraq").<sup id="cite_ref-347"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-347">[324]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-348"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-348">[325]</a></sup> Following [The Gambia's presidential election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2016_Gambian_presidential_election "2016 Gambian presidential election") later that year, which ended the long rule of [Yahya Jammeh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yahya_Jammeh "Yahya Jammeh"), the new government rescinded its withdrawal notification.<sup id="cite_ref-349"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-349">[326]</a></sup> The [High Court of South Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High_Court_of_South_Africa "High Court of South Africa") ruled on 2 February 2017 that the South African government's notice to withdraw was unconstitutional and invalid.<sup id="cite_ref-350"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-350">[327]</a></sup> On 7 March 2017 the South African government formally revoked its intention to withdraw.<sup id="cite_ref-351"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-351">[328]</a></sup> The ruling [ANC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_National_Congress "African National Congress") revealed on 5 July 2017 that its intention to withdraw stands.<sup id="cite_ref-352"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-352">[329]</a></sup>

### Philippines

Following the announcement that the ICC would open a preliminary investigation on the [Philippines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philippines "Philippines") in connection to its [escalating drug war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philippine_Drug_War "Philippine Drug War"), [President](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_the_Philippines "President of the Philippines") [Rodrigo Duterte](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rodrigo_Duterte "Rodrigo Duterte") announced on 14 March 2018 that the Philippines would start to submit plans to withdraw, completing the process on 17 March 2019. The ICC pointed out that it retained jurisdiction over the Philippines during the period when it was a state party to the Rome Statute, from November 2011 to March 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-353"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-353">[330]</a></sup>

### Russia

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/XV_BRICS_Summit_family_photo.jpg/220px-XV_BRICS_Summit_family_photo.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:XV_BRICS_Summit_family_photo.jpg)

To avoid arrest, Russian president [Vladimir Putin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Putin "Vladimir Putin") did not attend the [15th BRICS summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/15th_BRICS_summit "15th BRICS summit") in Johannesburg. Russia was represented by Foreign Minister [Sergei Lavrov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergei_Lavrov "Sergei Lavrov") (right).

In March 2023, Kremlin spokesperson [Dmitry Peskov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dmitry_Peskov "Dmitry Peskov") announced that [Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia "Russia") did not recognize the Court's decision to issue an arrest warrant for President [Vladimir Putin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Putin "Vladimir Putin") on account of [war crimes in Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine_(2022%E2%80%93present) "Russian invasion of Ukraine (2022–present)") and noted that Russia, like other countries which had not ratified the Rome Statute, did not recognise the jurisdiction of the ICC, saying "And accordingly, any decisions of this kind are null and void for the Russian Federation from the point of view of law."<sup id="cite_ref-:1_354-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-:1-354">[331]</a></sup>

[State Duma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_Duma "State Duma") speaker [Vyacheslav Volodin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vyacheslav_Volodin "Vyacheslav Volodin") wrote on [Telegram](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telegram_(software) "Telegram (software)"), "Yankees, hands off Putin!" calling the move evidence of Western "hysteria", and saying that "we regard any attacks on the President of the Russian Federation as aggression against our country".<sup id="cite_ref-:1_354-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-:1-354">[331]</a></sup>

South African Foreign Minister [Naledi Pandor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naledi_Pandor "Naledi Pandor") criticized the ICC for not having what she called an "evenhanded approach" to all leaders responsible for violations of international law.<sup id="cite_ref-355"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-355">[332]</a></sup> South Africa, which failed in its obligation to arrest visiting Sudanese President [Omar al-Bashir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Omar_al-Bashir "Omar al-Bashir") in June 2015, invited Vladimir Putin to the [15th BRICS Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BRICS#Summits "BRICS") in Durban.<sup id="cite_ref-356"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-356">[333]</a></sup> On 19 July 2023, South Africa announced that "by mutual agreement" Putin would not attend the summit. Foreign Minister [Sergei Lavrov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergei_Lavrov "Sergei Lavrov") attended in Putin's place.<sup id="cite_ref-357"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-357">[334]</a></sup>

In the months following the arrest warrant for Putin being issued, Russia issued warrants for the arrest of multiple ICC officials, including the court's president [Piotr Hofmański](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piotr_Hofma%C5%84ski "Piotr Hofmański") and its vice-president [Luz del Carmen Ibáñez Carranza](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luz_del_Carmen_Ib%C3%A1%C3%B1ez_Carranza "Luz del Carmen Ibáñez Carranza").<sup id="cite_ref-358"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-358">[335]</a></sup>

### United States government

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/President_George_W._Bush_signs_H.J._Resolution_114_authorizing_the_use_of_force_against_Iraq.jpg/220px-President_George_W._Bush_signs_H.J._Resolution_114_authorizing_the_use_of_force_against_Iraq.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:President_George_W._Bush_signs_H.J._Resolution_114_authorizing_the_use_of_force_against_Iraq.jpg)

U.S. President [George W. Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_W._Bush "George W. Bush") (left)

[President](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_the_United_States "President of the United States") [George W. Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_W._Bush "George W. Bush") signed the [American Service-Members' Protection Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Service-Members%27_Protection_Act "American Service-Members' Protection Act"), (informally referred to as _The Hague Invasion Act_), to signify the United States' opposition to any possible future jurisdiction of the court or its tribunals. The act gives the President the power to use "all means necessary and appropriate to bring about the release of any U.S. or allied personnel being detained or imprisoned by, on behalf of, or at the request of the International Criminal Court".<sup id="cite_ref-359"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-359">[336]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-360"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-360">[337]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-361"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-361">[338]</a></sup> During the administration of [Barack Obama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barack_Obama "Barack Obama"), U.S. opposition to the ICC evolved to "positive engagement", although no effort was made to [ratify](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ratify "Ratify") the Rome Statute.<sup id="cite_ref-362"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-362">[339]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-363"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-363">[340]</a></sup>

The subsequent [Donald Trump administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidency_of_Donald_Trump "Presidency of Donald Trump") was considerably more hostile to the Court, similar to the Bush administration threatening prosecution and financial sanctions on ICC judges and staff in U.S. courts as well as imposing visa bans in response to any investigation against American nationals in connection to alleged crimes and atrocities perpetrated by the U.S. in Afghanistan. The threat included sanctions against any of over 120 countries which have ratified the Court for cooperating in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-364"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-364">[341]</a></sup> In November 2017, [Fatou Bensouda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fatou_Bensouda "Fatou Bensouda") advised the court to consider seeking charges for human rights abuses committed during the [War in Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%932021) "War in Afghanistan (2001–2021)") such as alleged rapes and tortures by the [U.S. Armed Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/U.S._Armed_Forces "U.S. Armed Forces") and the [Central Intelligence Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency "Central Intelligence Agency"), crime against humanity committed by the [Taliban](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taliban "Taliban"), and war crimes committed by the [Afghan National Security Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_National_Security_Forces "Afghan National Security Forces").<sup id="cite_ref-365"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-365">[342]</a></sup> [John Bolton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_R._Bolton "John R. Bolton"), [National Security Advisor of the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Security_Advisor_of_the_United_States "National Security Advisor of the United States"), stated that ICC Court had no jurisdiction over the U.S., which did not ratify the [Rome Statute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rome_Statute "Rome Statute").<sup id="cite_ref-366"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-366">[343]</a></sup> In 2020, overturning the previous decision not to proceed, senior judges at the ICC authorized an investigation into the alleged war crimes in Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-367"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-367">[344]</a></sup>

On 11 June 2020, the United States announced sanctions on officials and employees, as well as their families, involved in investigating alleged crimes against humanity committed by U.S. armed forces in Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-368"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-368">[345]</a></sup> This move was widely criticized by human rights groups.<sup id="cite_ref-369"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-369">[346]</a></sup> The U.S. ordered sanctions against the ICC prosecutor Fatou Bensouda, and the ICC's head of Jurisdiction, Complementary, and Cooperation Division, Phakiso Mochochok, for an investigation into alleged war crimes by U.S. forces and the Central Intelligence Agency (CIA) in Afghanistan since 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-370"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-370">[347]</a></sup> "The US government has reason to doubt the honesty of the ICC. The Department of Justice has received substantial credible information that raises serious concerns about a long history of financial corruption and malfeasance at the highest levels of the office of the prosecutor", Attorney General [William Barr](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Barr "William Barr") stated. The ICC responded with a statement expressing "profound regret at the announcement of further threats and coercive actions." "These attacks constitute an escalation and an unacceptable attempt to interfere with the rule of law and the Court's judicial proceedings", the statement said. "They are announced with the declared aim of influencing the actions of ICC officials in the context of the court's independent and objective investigations and impartial judicial proceedings."<sup id="cite_ref-371"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-371">[348]</a></sup>

On 30 September 2020, prominent United States human rights lawyers announced that they would sue Trump and his Administration—including [Secretary of State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secretary_of_State "Secretary of State") [Mike Pompeo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mike_Pompeo "Mike Pompeo"), Treasury secretary [Steven Mnuchin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steven_Mnuchin "Steven Mnuchin"), attorney general [William Barr](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Barr "William Barr"), and [OFAC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Office_of_Foreign_Assets_Control "Office of Foreign Assets Control") director [Andrea Gacki](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrea_Gacki "Andrea Gacki"), and the departments they head—on the grounds that Trump's [Executive Order 13928](https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Executive_Order_13928 "s:Executive Order 13928") order had [gagged them](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gag_order "Gag order"), violating their right to free speech and impeding their work in trying to obtain justice on behalf of victims of war crimes. One of the plaintiffs, [Diane Marie Amann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diane_Marie_Amann "Diane Marie Amann"), stated that, as a result of sanctions against the chief prosecutor at the ICC, she herself risked having her family assets seized if she continued to work for children who are bought and sold by traffickers, killed, tortured, sexually abused and forced to become child soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-372"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-372">[349]</a></sup>

On 4 January 2021, U.S. District Judge [Katherine Polk Failla](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Katherine_Polk_Failla "Katherine Polk Failla") in New York City issued a preliminary injunction against the Trump administration from imposing criminal or civil penalties against ICC personnel and those who support the court's work, including the plaintiffs.<sup id="cite_ref-373"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-373">[350]</a></sup> The sanctions were subsequently lifted by the Biden administration Secretary of State [Antony Blinken](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antony_Blinken "Antony Blinken") in April 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-374"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-374">[351]</a></sup>

In 2024, the Biden administration opposed an [arrest warrant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court_investigation_in_Palestine "International Criminal Court investigation in Palestine") for Israeli Prime Minister [Benjamin Netanyahu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benjamin_Netanyahu "Benjamin Netanyahu") over [Israeli war crimes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israeli_war_crimes "Israeli war crimes") committed during the [Israel–Hamas war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel%E2%80%93Hamas_war "Israel–Hamas war") in the [Gaza Strip](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaza_Strip "Gaza Strip").<sup id="cite_ref-375"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-375">[352]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-376"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-376">[353]</a></sup>

#### U.S. criticisms

The [United States Department of State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State "United States Department of State") argues that there are "insufficient checks and balances on the authority of the ICC prosecutor and judges" and "insufficient protection against politicized prosecutions or other abuses".<sup id="cite_ref-US_DoS_faq_80-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-US_DoS_faq-80">[80]</a></sup> The current law in the United States on the ICC is the _[American Service-Members' Protection Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Service-Members%27_Protection_Act "American Service-Members' Protection Act")_ (ASPA), 116 Stat. 820. The ASPA authorizes the President of the United States to use "all means necessary and appropriate to bring about the release of any U.S. or allied personnel being detained or imprisoned by, on behalf of, or at the request of the International Criminal Court". This authorization has led the act to be nicknamed the "Hague Invasion Act",<sup id="cite_ref-377"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-377">[354]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-378"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-378">[355]</a></sup> because the freeing of U.S. citizens by force might be possible only through military action.

On 10 September 2018, [John R. Bolton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_R._Bolton "John R. Bolton"), in his first major address as U.S. [National Security Advisor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Security_Advisor_(United_States) "National Security Advisor (United States)"), reiterated that the ICC lacks checks and balances, exercises "jurisdiction over crimes that have disputed and ambiguous definitions", and has failed to "deter and punish atrocity crimes". The ICC, Bolton said, was "superfluous", given that "domestic judicial systems already hold American citizens to the highest legal and ethical standards". He added that the U.S. would do everything "to protect our citizens" should the ICC attempt to prosecute U.S. servicemen over alleged [detainee abuse in Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bagram_torture_and_prisoner_abuse "Bagram torture and prisoner abuse"). In that event, ICC judges and prosecutors would be barred from entering the U.S., their funds in the U.S. would be [sanctioned](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanctions_(law) "Sanctions (law)") and the U.S. "will prosecute them in the U.S. criminal system. We will do the same for any company or state that assists an ICC investigation of Americans", Bolton said. He also criticized [Palestinian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palestinian_government "Palestinian government") efforts to bring [Israel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel "Israel") before the ICC over allegations of [human rights](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_rights "Human rights") abuses in the [West Bank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Bank "West Bank") and [Gaza](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaza_Strip "Gaza Strip").<sup id="cite_ref-379"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-379">[356]</a></sup>

### OPCD

Concerning the independent Office of Public Counsel for the Defence (OPCD), Thomas Lubanga's defence team say they were given a smaller budget than the Prosecutor and that evidence and witness statements were slow to arrive.<sup id="cite_ref-cfr_124-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-cfr-124">[124]</a></sup>

### Impartiality

Human Rights Watch (HRW) reported that the ICC's prosecutor team takes no account of the roles played by the government in the conflict of Uganda, Rwanda or Congo. This led to a flawed investigation, because the ICC did not reach the conclusion of its verdict after considering the governments' position and actions in the conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-380"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-380">[357]</a></sup>

### Unintentional consequences

Research indicates that prosecutions of leaders who are culpable of international crimes in the ICC makes them less likely to peacefully step down, which can prolong conflicts and incentivize them to make continued use of mass violence.<sup id="cite_ref-381"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-381">[358]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-382"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-382">[359]</a></sup> It is also argued that justice is a means to peace: "As a result, the ICC has been used as a means of intervention in ongoing conflicts with the expectation that the indictments, arrests, and trials of elite perpetrators have deterrence and preventive effects for atrocity crimes. Despite these legitimate intentions and great expectations, there is little evidence of the efficacy of justice as a means to peace".<sup id="cite_ref-383"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-383">[360]</a></sup>

### State cooperation

That the ICC cannot mount successful cases without state cooperation is problematic for several reasons. It means that the ICC acts inconsistently in its selection of cases, is prevented from taking on hard cases and loses legitimacy.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_384-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-:0-384">[361]</a></sup> It also gives the ICC less deterrent value, as potential perpetrators of war crimes know that they can avoid ICC judgment by taking over government and refusing to cooperate.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_384-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-:0-384">[361]</a></sup>

### Principle of complementarity

The fundamental principle of complementarity of the ICC Rome Statute is often taken for granted in the legal analysis of international criminal law and its jurisprudence. Initially the thorny issue of the actual application of the complementarity principle arose in 2008, when [William Schabas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Schabas "William Schabas") published his influential paper.<sup id="cite_ref-385"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-385">[362]</a></sup> No substantive research was made by other scholars on this issue for quite some time. In June 2017, Victor Tsilonis advanced the same criticism which is reinforced by events, practices of the Office of the Prosecutor and ICC cases in the Essays in Honour of [Nestor Courakis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nestor_Courakis "Nestor Courakis"). His paper essentially argues that the Αl‐Senussi case arguably is the first instance of the complementarity principle's actual implementation eleven whole years after the ratification of the Rome Statute of the International Criminal Court.<sup id="cite_ref-386"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-386">[363]</a></sup>

On the other hand, in 2017, Chief Prosecutor Fatou Bensouda invoked the principle of complementarity in the situation between Russia and Georgia in the [Ossetia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ossetia "Ossetia") region.<sup id="cite_ref-387"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-387">[364]</a></sup> Moreover, following the threats of certain African states (initially Burundi, Gambia and South Africa) to withdraw their ratifications, Bensouda again referred to the principle of complementarity as a core principle of ICC's jurisdiction and has more extensively focused on the principle's application on the latest Office of The Prosecutor's Report on Preliminary Examination Activities 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-388"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-388">[365]</a></sup>

Some advocates have suggested that the ICC go "beyond complementarity" and systematically support national capacity for prosecutions.<sup id="cite_ref-389"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-389">[366]</a></sup> They argue that national prosecutions, where possible, are more cost-effective, preferable to victims and more sustainable.

### Jurisdiction over corporations

There is a debate on whether the ICC should have jurisdiction over [corporations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corporation "Corporation") that violate international law.<sup id="cite_ref-390"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-390">[367]</a></sup> Supporters argue that corporations can and do commit human rights violations,<sup id="cite_ref-carrillo-santarelli_391-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-carrillo-santarelli-391">[368]</a></sup> such as war crimes linked to raw materials in conflict zones.<sup id="cite_ref-392"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-392">[369]</a></sup> Critics argue that prosecuting corporations would compromise the principle of complementarity,<sup id="cite_ref-393"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-393">[370]</a></sup> that it would give corporations excessive power under international law, or that it would compromise voluntary initiatives by companies.<sup id="cite_ref-carrillo-santarelli_391-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-carrillo-santarelli-391">[368]</a></sup> [John Ruggie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Ruggie "John Ruggie") has argued that jurisdiction of corporations under international law should be limited to international crimes, while Nicolás Carrillo-Santarelli of [La Sabana University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_La_Sabana "University of La Sabana") argues that it should cover all human rights violations.<sup id="cite_ref-394"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-394">[371]</a></sup>

Despite its lack of jurisdiction, the ICC announced in 2016 that it would prioritize criminal cases linked to land grabbing, illegal resource extraction, or [environmental degradation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Environmental_degradation "Environmental degradation") caused by corporate activity.<sup id="cite_ref-395"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-395">[372]</a></sup> The proposed crime of [ecocide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecocide "Ecocide") would have jurisdiction over corporations as well as governments.<sup id="cite_ref-time_396-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-time-396">[373]</a></sup> Supporters of criminalizing ecocide argue that it would shift the ICC's priorities away from Africa, since most [environmental degradation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Environmental_degradation "Environmental degradation") is caused by states and corporations in the Global North.<sup id="cite_ref-time_396-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_note-time-396">[373]</a></sup>

242.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_ref-264 "Jump up")** [ICC case information sheet on the Ntaganda case](https://www.icc-cpi.int/CaseInformationSheets/NtagandaEng.pdf). Retrieved 9 July 2022.
243.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_ref-265 "Jump up")** [Order regarding the hearing scheduled for 29 June - 1 July 2020](https://www.icc-cpi.int/CourtRecords/CR2020_02284.PDF). Retrieved 11 June 2020.
244.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_ref-266 "Jump up")** Article 110 (3) of the [Rome Statute of the International Court](http://www.icc-cpi.int/NR/rdonlyres/ADD16852-AEE9-4757-ABE7-9CDC7CF02886/283503/RomeStatutEng1.pdf) states that "\[w\]hen the person has served two thirds of the sentence, or 25 years in the case of life imprisonment, the Court shall review the sentence to determine whether it should be reduced. Such a review shall not be conducted before that time." Article 78 (3) of the Rome Statute specifies that "\[i\]n imposing a sentence of imprisonment, the Court shall deduct the time, if any, previously spent in detention in accordance with an order of the Court. The Court may deduct any time otherwise spent in detention in connection with conduct underlying the crime." The Court's Trial Chamber VI determined in its sentencing decision that the time since 22 March 2013 is to be deducted from the sentence. Thus, Bosco Ntaganda is to be released on or before 22 March 2043. Starting from 22 March 2013, two-thirds of thirty years (twenty years) will elapse on 22 March 2033.

277.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_ref-300 "Jump up")** ["Al-Mahdi Case (The Prosecutor v. Ahmad Al Faqi Al Mahdi)"](https://www.icc-cpi.int/mali/al-mahdi). _International Criminal Court_.
278.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_ref-301 "Jump up")** [ICC case information sheet on the al-Mahdi case](https://www.icc-cpi.int/CaseInformationSheets/Al-MahdiEng.pdf). Retrieved 11 June 2020.
279.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court#cite_ref-302 "Jump up")** Article 110 (3) of the [Rome Statute of the International Court](http://www.icc-cpi.int/NR/rdonlyres/ADD16852-AEE9-4757-ABE7-9CDC7CF02886/283503/RomeStatutEng1.pdf) states that "\[w\]hen the person has served two thirds of the sentence, or 25 years in the case of life imprisonment, the Court shall review the sentence to determine whether it should be reduced. Such a review shall not be conducted before that time." Article 78 (3) of the Rome Statute specifies that "\[i\]n imposing a sentence of imprisonment, the Court shall deduct the time, if any, previously spent in detention in accordance with an order of the Court. The Court may deduct any time otherwise spent in detention in connection with conduct underlying the crime." The Court's Trial Chamber VIII determined in its [sentencing decision](https://www.icc-cpi.int/CourtRecords/CR2016_07244.PDF) that the time since 18 September 2015 is to be deducted from the sentence. Thus, Ahmad Al-Mahdi is to be released on or before 18 September 2024. Starting from 18 September 2015, two-thirds of nine years (six years) will elapse on 18 September 2021.
